<div
class="header
artist_info_header
int-1
clearfix">














<h1
class="hed-1"
title="George
Strait"
itemprop="name">George
Strait</h1>














<div
class="header_btns">















<div
class="bookmark"
data-musicid="R16775"></div>














</div>













</div>













<div
class="int-1">














<div
class="content_right">















<div
class="artist_bio_container
clearfix">
















<div
class="artist_bio
short_bio">
















<div
class="artist_bio_inner">

















Out
of
all
the
new
country
singers
to
emerge
in
the
early
'80s,
George
Strait
stayed
the
closest
to
traditional
country.
Drawing
from
both
the
honky
tonk
and
Western
swing
traditions,
Strait
didn't
refashion
the
genres;
instead,
he
revitalized
them
for
a
new
decade.
In
the
process,
he
became
one
of
the
most
popular
and
influential
singers
of
the
decade,
sparking
a
wave
of
neo-traditionalist
singers
from


















<a
href="/randy-travis"
class="hash">Randy
Travis</a>
and


















<a
href="/dwight-yoakam"
class="hash">Dwight
Yoakam</a>
to


















<a
href="/clint-black"
class="hash">Clint
Black</a>,


















<a
href="/garth-brooks"
class="hash">Garth
Brooks</a>,
and


















<a
href="/alan-jackson"
class="hash">Alan
Jackson</a>.


















<br
/>

















<br
/>
Strait
was
born
and
raised
in
Texas,
the
son
of
a
junior
high
school
teacher
who
also
owned
and
operated
a
ranch
that
had
been
in
the
Strait
family
for
nearly
100
years.
When
George
was
a
child,
his
mother
left
the
family,
taking
her
daughter
but
leaving
her
sons
behind
with
their
father.
During
his
childhood,
he
would
spend
his
weekdays
in
town
and
his
weekends
on
the
ranch.
Strait
began
playing
music
as
a
teenager,
joining
a
rock
&amp;
roll
garage
band.
After
his
high
school
graduation
in
the
late
'60s,
he
enrolled
in
college
but
soon
dropped
out
and
eloped
with
his
high
school
sweetheart,
Norma.
In
1971,
Strait
enlisted
in
the
Army;
two
years
later,
he
was
stationed
in
Hawaii.
While
there,
he
began
playing
country
music,
initially
with
an
Army-sponsored
country
band
called
Rambling
Country.
They
played
several
dates
off
the
base
under
the
name


















<i>Santee</i>.
Strait
left
the
Army
in
1975,
returning
to
Texas
with
the
intent
of
completing
his
education.
He
enrolled
in
Southwest
Texas
State
University
at
San
Marcos,
where
he
studied
agriculture.
While
he
was
studying,
he
formed
his
own
country
band,
Ace
in
the
Hole.


















<br
/>

















<br
/>
Ace
in
the
Hole
made
a
few
records
for
the
independent
Dallas-based
label
D
in
the
late
'70s,
but
they
never
went
anywhere.
Toward
the
end
of
the
decade,
Strait
attempted
to
carve
out
a
niche
in
Nashville,
but
he
failed
since
he
lacked
any
strong
connections.
In
1979,
he
became
friends
with
Erv
Woolsey,
a
Texas
club
owner
who
had
formerly
worked
for
MCA
Records.
Woolsey
had
several
MCA
executives
come
down
to
Texas
to
hear
Strait.
His
performance
convinced
the
company
to
sign
him
in
1980.
&quot;Unwound,&quot;
Strait's
first
single,
was
released
in
the
spring
of
1981
and
climbed
into
the
Top
Ten.
The
follow-up,
&quot;Down
and
Out,&quot;
stalled
at
16,
but
&quot;If
You're
Thinking
You
Want
a
Stranger
(There's
One
Coming
Home)&quot;
reached
number
three
in
early
1982.
The
song
sparked
a
remarkable
string
of
Top
Ten
hits
that
ran
well
into
the
'90s.
During
that
time
he
had
an
astonishing
31
number
one
singles,
beginning
with
1982's
&quot;Fool
Hearted
Memory.&quot;


















<br
/>

















<br
/>
Throughout
the
'80s,
he
dominated
the
country
singles
charts,
and
his
albums
consistently
went
platinum
or
gold.
Strait
rarely
abandoned
hardcore
honky
tonk
and
Western
swing
--
toward
the
beginning
of
the
'90s,
his
sound
became
a
little
slicker,
but
it
was
only
a
relative
change.
Strait
was
also
one
of
the
few
'80s
superstars
to
survive
the
generational
shift
of
the
early
'90s
that
began
with
the
phenomenal
success
of


















<a
href="/garth-brooks"
class="hash">Garth
Brooks</a>.
In
1992,
he
made
his
first
movie,
Pure
Country,
which
featured
him
in
the
lead
role.
Strait
released
a
four-disc
box
set
career
retrospective,
Strait
Out
of
the
Box,
in
1995.
By
the
spring
of
1996,
it
had
become
one
of
the
five
biggest-selling
box
sets
in
popular
music
history.
Blue
Clear
Sky,
his
1996
album,
debuted
on
the
country
charts
at
number
one
and
the
pop
charts
at
number
seven.
In
1997,
he
released


















<a
href="/george-strait/carrying-your-love-with-me"
class="hash">Carrying
Your
Love
with
Me</a>,
following
it
with


















<a
href="/george-strait/1-step-at-time"
class="hash">One
Step
at
a
Time</a>
in
1998.


















<a
href="/george-strait/always-never-same"
class="hash">Always
Never
the
Same</a>
appeared
a
year
later,
as
did
the
seasonal
effort


















<a
href="/george-strait-holiday/merry-christmas-wherever-you-are"
class="hash">Merry
Christmas
Wherever
You
Are</a>.
The
simply
titled


















<a
href="/george-strait/george-strait"
class="hash">George
Strait</a>,
featuring
the
hit
single
&quot;Go
On,&quot;
hit
the
shelves
in
late
2000.


















<br
/>

















<br
/>
Did
Strait
slow
down?
Nah.
The
following
year
saw
the
release
of


















<a
href="/george-strait/road-less-traveled"
class="hash">The
Road
Less
Traveled</a>,
which
qualified
as
an
experimental
album
of
sorts
for
the
veteran
performer.
While
it
didn't
stray
very
far
from
his
new
traditionalist
country
sound,


















<a
href="/george-strait/road-less-traveled"
class="hash">Road</a>
did
include
a
foray
into
vocal
processing
that
was
about
as
country
as
a
pair
of
stiletto-healed
cowboy
boots.
But
the
experimentation
was
welcome,
for
it
revealed
that
Strait
was
still
hungry,
even
after
millions
upon
millions
of
records
sold.
Strait
issued
two
projects
in
2003.


















<a
href="#"
class="hash">For
the
Last
Time:
Live
from
the
Astrodome</a>
chronicled
his
headlining
set
at
the
last
Houston
Livestock
and
Rodeo
ever
held
in
the
big
Texas
dome,
while


















<a
href="/george-strait/honkytonkville"
class="hash">Honkytonkville</a>
was
a
fiery
set
of
hard
country,
lauded
by
critics
for
its
mixture
of
the
old
Strait
with
his
modern,
superstar
self.


















<a
href="/george-strait/somewhere-down-in-texas"
class="hash">Somewhere
Down
in
Texas</a>
arrived
in
2005,
followed
by


















<a
href="/george-strait/it-just-comes-natural"
class="hash">It
Just
Comes
Natural</a>
in
2006,
and


















<a
href="/george-strait/troubadour"
class="hash">Troubadour</a>
and
the
holiday
album


















<a
href="/george-strait-holiday/fresh-cut-christmas"
class="hash">Classic
Christmas</a>
in
2008.


















<a
href="/george-strait/twang"
class="hash">Twang</a>,
co-produced
by
Strait
and
Tony
Brown,
appeared
in
2009.
Strait
co-wrote
seven
of
the
eleven
songs
(sharing
credits
with
Dean
Dillon,
Bobby
Boyd,
and
his
son,
Bubba
Strait)
on
2011's


















<a
href="/george-strait/here-for-good-time"
class="hash">Here
for
a
Good
Time</a>,
his
39th
studio
album,
which
was
co-produced
by
Strait
and
Brown
and
recorded
at


















<a
href="/jimmy-buffett"
class="hash">Jimmy
Buffett</a>'s
Shrimpboat
Sound
Studio
in
Key
West,
Florida.


















<br
/>

















<br
/>


















<a
href="/george-strait/here-for-good-time"
class="hash">Here
for
a
Good
Time</a>
gave
Strait
two
more
Top
10
singles
--
&quot;Here
for
a
Good
Time&quot;
and
&quot;Love's
Gonna
Make
It
Alright&quot;
both
missed
the
top
of
the
charts,
peaking
at
two
and
three,
respectively
--
and
about
a
year
after
its
release,
Strait
announced
that
he
was
retiring
from
touring.
He
planned
one
last
tour,
called
The
Cowboy
Rides
Away
Tour,
beginning
in
2013
and
ending
in
2014.
Just
before
this
farewell
journey
kicked
off,
Strait
released
a
new
album,


















<a
href="/george-strait/love-is-everything"
class="hash">Love
Is
Everything</a>,
in
May
of
2013;
it
was
preceded
by
the
single
&quot;Give
It
All
We
Got
Tonight.&quot;
~
Stephen
Thomas
Erlewine,
Rovi
















</div>















</div>
















<a
href="#"
class="full_bio_link">full
bio</a>















</div>
